Ladies and Gentlemen: 
This report presents the data collected and results of the drilled shaft load test 
program performed at the location designated as Load Test Shaft LT-A in the Ewa 
Plains for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) in the District 
of Ewa on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
Please note that the soil samples recovered during our field exploration 
(remaining after testing) will be stored for a period of two months from the date of this 
report. The samples will be discarded after that date unless arrangements are made for 
a longer storage period. Please contact our office for alternative sample storage 
requirements, if appropriate. 
If you have questions or need additional information, please contact our office. 
Very truly yours, 
GEOLABS, INC.

SECTION 1.  OBJECTIVES 
The Hon olulu High-Capacity Transit Co
rridor Project (HHCT CP) involves the 
construction and operation of a fixed-guideway transit system  in the corridor between 
Kapolei and the University of Hawaii at Manoa with a branch to Waikiki on the Island of 
Oahu, Hawaii. This report pr esents the results of the dr
illed s haft load test program 
performed at a load test shaf t, designated as LT-A, along t he fixed-guideway alignment 
in the vicinity of the future Ho`opili St
ation in the Ewa area 
on the Island of Oahu, 
Hawaii. The project location 
and general vicinity are show
n on the Project Location 
Map, Plate 1. 
The proposed high-capacity transit system will  have an elevated fixed-guidewa y 
system wit h a ty pical 150-f oot long s pan be tween bent columns. Re latively large axia l 
loads and high overturning moments typically are generated by these long span structures 
with large live loads. We understand non-redundant drilled shaft foundations (single drilled 
shaft supporting each bent co
lumn) have been co nsidered for support  of the elevated 
guideway structure planned for the project to reduce the foundation footprint and to reduce 
the need for extensive relocation of the existing underground utilities. 
In general, drilled s
haft foundations wo
uld deriv e vertical support from a
combination of skin friction between the 
drilled shaft and the surrounding subsurface
materials and from end bearing at the base 
of the drilled shaft. The drilled shaft 
foundations would derive a large portion of the bearing support from adhesion between 
the sides of the drilled shaft and the surroundi ng soils and/or rock formation. Therefore, 
the adhesion values and end-bearing contribution used in the analyses would hav e a 
significant impact on the lengths of the dr illed shaft foundations p lanned for the project 
design. 
In order to provide ad ditional information and data for the drille d shaft foundation 
design, we have undertaken a drilled shaft load test program, including the performance 
of an instrumented load test, to fulfill the following objectives: 
To evalu ate the adh esion an d end- bearing values  of the drilled sh
aft 
embedded in the older alluvium deposits at the load test site.

 the relatively large diameter of

SECTION 2.  SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our drilled shaft load test  program was to evaluat e the adhesion 
and end-bearing values of the older alluv
ium deposits anticipated in the Ewa Plains 
area of the project alignment. Our work was performed in gener al accordance with the 
Agreement for Professional Services entered into on June 30, 2009 with the City and 
County of Honolulu, Department of Transportation Services. In order to accomplish this, 
we conduc ted a drilled shaft load test program
 at the LT-A location consis ting of the 
following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available in-house geologic and soils information 
in the project vicinity.  
2. 
Coordination with D.R. Horton to obt ain the Right-of-Entr y permit prior to 
the field activities at the load test site situated within their property. 
3. 
Coordination of utilit y toning and  clearance  of the boring and lo
ad test 
shaft locations with various utility companies. 
4. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill ri g and operators  
to and from the project site. 
5. 
Drilling and sampling of two (2) borings extending to  depths of about 86.5 
and 62.4 feet below the existing ground
 surface for a total of about 
149 lineal feet of exploration. 
6. 
Backfilling of the drille d borings with bentonite chips follo wed by hydrating 
the bentonite chips. 
7. 
Coordination of the field explor
ation and logging of the borings by our
geologist. 
8. 
Laboratory testing of selected soil 
samples retrieved from our field 
exploration as an aid in class
ifying the materials and evalu
ating their  
engineering properties. 
9. 
Excavation of an 8-foot nomi
nal diameter load test shaft by our
subcontractor, CMZ of Hawaii, Inc., ex tending to a depth of approximately 
60.5 feet below the existing ground surface. 
10. 
Performance of a drilled shaft base inspection utilizing a Miniature Shaft  
Inspection Device (Mini-SID) to check the base of the drille
d shaft for 
cleanliness when using the end-bear
ing component for foundation 
support.

SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu generally is com
posed of the weathered and diss
ected 
remnants of two extinct shield (domed)
 volcanoes: Waianae Volcano and Koola
u 
Volcano. The older Waianae Volcano forms th
e bulk of the wester
n third of Oahu’s 
landmass while the younger Koolau Volc
ano forms a major portion of the eastern 
two-thirds of Oahu’s l andmass. Generally, it is believ ed that the Waianae and Koola u 
Shield Volcanoes began erupting on the order of about 20 to
 25 million y ears ago a s 
deep ocean submarine volcanoes. Subaerial volcanic eruptions on the Island of Oahu 
ended a pproximately 2.2 to 3.5 
million years ago in the
 Ple istocene Epoc h. 
Furthermore, the Waianae Volcano became ex tinct while the Koolau Volcano remained 
active, therefore, the eastern fl
ank of Waianae Volc ano is par tially overlapped and 
buried below the younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior portions of the 
Island of Oahu. 
In addition to the pri
mary volcanic co mposition of the Is land of Oahu, other  
secondary and generally more localized geologic earth materials comprise the lowland, 
coastal, and valley portions of the island. The secondary earth materials include various 
alluvial and colluvial deposits, oceanic and marine sediments, submerged and emerged 
coralline reef materials, and surface fills. 
Load Test Shaft LT-A is situated in the Ewa Plain (in the lower distal flanks of the 
Waianae and Koolau Mountain Ranges). The Ew a Plain is a gently sloping land surface 
formed by the long-term accumulation of allu
vial s ediments primarily consisting of 
terrigenous clays and silts with some embedded cobbles and boulders derived from the 
weathering and decomposition of basaltic rock residing further upslope. Some pockets 
of marine sediments and corallin e detritus materials were intercalated within the alluvial 
sediments as a result of past sea-level fluctuations. 
The Ewa Plain is dis sected by various  drainage gullies, which emanate in
 the 
upland areas and dis charge into the Middle and West Lochs of Pearl Harbor . With the 
passage of time, some of the incised dr ainage channels meandered in course and were 
LT-A  11/19/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. Page 
6 
Hawaii ● California 
filled-in by more recent soft alluvial sedi ments. The deeper basaltic rock of the Waianae 
and Koolau Mountain Ranges is  the primar y aquifer and potable water source on the 
Island of Oahu. The subsurface geologic
al c onditions at the load test shaft location 
reflect past glacio-eustatic sea-level fluct
uations, which occurred most substantially 
during the Pleistocene Epoch. 
The subsurface geological conditions at the load test shaft location (LT-A) are 
typified by  various terrigenous  alluv ial deposits with some embedded cobbles and 
boulders, buried volcanic materials consisti
ng of residual and s
aprolitic soils and 
weathered basaltic rock formation. The base of the stratigraphic section in this area is  
the volcanic materials associated with the Waianae and Koolau Basalt. 
During the Pleistocene Epoch ( most recent Ice Age), many sea-level fluctuations 
occurred because of the waxing and waning of global continental glaciation. Generally 
speaking, as the great continen tal glaciers accumulated, the global level of  the oceans 
fell because there was less water available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the 
glaciers receded (melted), the global level of the oceans ro
se because of the water 
influx. The landmass elevation of the Isl and of Oahu remained e ssentially stable during 
these sea-level changes; therefore, the fl
uctuations were eustatic in nature. The 
glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of  the sea that were either higher or lower  
relative to the present sea-level condi
tions. The following are some generaliz
ed 
examples of the geomorphic effe cts that resulted from the changes in sea level relative 
to the island landmass. 
The higher sea-level st ands caused the accumulation of deltas and depos itional 
fans composed of terrigenous s ediments at  the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea  stands caused accumulation of co ralline reef materials at correspond ingly 
higher elev ations and the depos ition of lagoonal and mari
ne sediments in the quiet
backwaters protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level s tands caused stream s to incise erosiona l valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef plat forms. In addition, the subaer ial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcar eous materials caused c onsolidation of the soft deltaic materials  
LT-A  11/19/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. Page 
7 
Hawaii ● California 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardeni
ng of the calcareous re
ef materials. Finally,  
renewed subaerial erosion (acti ng on the upper regions of t he volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energ y 
depositional environments, which is  typified by a poorly  sorted mix of alluvial materials 
with a greater range in particle sizes. During periods of no significant sea level change, 
continued stream flow extende d the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the 
deposition of alluvium overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
Agricultural land use and other developm
ent projects at the load test shaft 
location have resulted in alterati ons to the natural terrain including the placement of 
earth fills, which may be of poor quality in terms of supporting large structural loads. 
3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed one stratigraphic unit  of Older Alluvium (Qa) at the l oad 
test shaft location (LT-A). The followin
g section briefly describes the general 
characteristics of the above stratigraphic
 unit based on literature research, past 
experience with similar mate
rials, and the field explor
ation and laboratory testing 
program conducted at the load test shaft location. 
3.2.1 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits  are generally very st iff to hard silty and clayey soils that
originally were deposited in alluvial env
ironments, buried over  long peri ods of  
time by more recent deposits to bec
ome somewhat consolidated, and then 
subsequently uncovered at the ground surf
ace by erosion or other geologic 
processes. This unit generally
 overlies 
the Koolau Basalt and consists of 
terrigenous sediments, which have been tr ansported by stream  action from the 
upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried str eam channels sometimes are encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles  and 
boulders. These coarse-grained portions of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
LT-A  11/19/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. Page 
8 
Hawaii ● California 
may resemble saprolit e soil deposits but ar e clearly differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally ex
hibit high strength, whic
h leads to a 
relatively high bearing or load carryi
ng capacity with generally low settlement 
characteristics. Frequently, these materi als show moderate to high shrink/swel l 
potential when subjected to fluctuations in the moisture contents. However, this is 
not considered a constraint for the type of
 structural system s proposed for this  
project. Structurally, the Older Alluvium materials are considered to be sufficiently 
competent to support relatively large 
structural loads with low settlement 
characteristics when designed properly. 
3.2.2 Groundwat er 
The load test shaft location  (LT-A ) i s wi thin the S outhern Oahu regional  aqui fer 
system, which is the largest basal groundwater area on the Island of Oahu. Koolau 
Basalt is t he primary aquifer in t he central and east ern parts of the area, whereas 
the Waianae Volcanics forms the main aquifer in the wester n part. Unconsolidated 
and consolidated sedimentary deposits form a thick confining unit (“caprock”) near 
the coast. 
Water levels i n the Souther n Oahu groundwater area ge nerally range from about 
25 to 30 feet above sea level inland to about 15 to 20 feet above sea level near the 
shore where the water is under artesian pressure because it is confined by caprock. 
The caprock impedes the seaward movement of fresh groundwater. In the eastern 
part of the area, thick valley fill and underlying weathered rocks form partial barriers 
to groundwater flow. I n the west ern part of the ar ea, the weat hered zone near t he 
unconformity separating Koolau Basal t from the unde
rlying Waianae V olcanics 
impedes the flow of water between the two volcanic rock aquifers.

were explored by drilling and sampling two  borings, designated as Boring No. 1 (B-1) 
and Boring No. 1A (B-1A), at the approximate locations shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2.

drilled boring extending from 
depths of about 10 to
15 feet below the existing ground surface. 
We did not encounter groundwater in the two borings drilled at the load test shaft 
location (LT-A) at the time of our field exploration. It should be noted that groundwater  
levels could fluctuate depending on seasonal
 precipitation, gr oundwater withdrawal  
and/or injection, and other factors.

SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM 
The drilled shaft load test program conduct ed at the load test shaft LT-A location 
of the HHCTCP generally c
onsisted of t
he following field
 and laboratory work 
components. A brief description of each work  component is presented below. Detailed  
discussions, findings, and the test results of the various components are provided in the 
respective appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings and Associated Field Testing 
Soil s ampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in t
he 
introduction to Appendix A.
 T he following discussion provide
s an overview of the 
methods and procedures utilized in the test boring exploration. 
The two s oil borings  were adv anced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight
solid-stem augers. Sampling of the soils wa
s performed using the modified California 
sampler (ASTM D 3550) and the Standard Penet ration Test (SPT) split spoon sampler 
(ASTM D 1586). 
In-situ testing of the subsurface material s in the borings includ ed the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586.  SPT sampling provides a measure of 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
is defined as the number of blows requi
red to drive a standard split-spoon sampler
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 in
ches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 in
ches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. 
During the field exploration progr
am, our geologist performed pocket 
penetrometer tests on selected cohesive soil 
samples retrieved. Pocket penetrometer 
tests provide an indication of the unconfined compressive strength of a soil sample. The 
pocket penetrometer test values are shown on the Logs of Borings in Appendix A.

4.2 
Laboratory Tests 
Laboratory tests were performed on sele
cted soil samples to determine the 
physical and engineering properties. Laborat
ory tests performed included natural 
moisture content, in-situ unit weight, Atte
rberg limits, and unconsolidated-undrained 
(UU) triaxial compression. T he laboratory test results ar e summarized on the logs of
borings, where appropriate, and graphic presentati on of the test result s are provided in 
Appendix B. 
4.3 
Load Test Shaft Installation 
An 8-foot nomi nal diameter drill ed shaft (designated as LT-A ) was excavated to a 
depth of approxim ately 60. 5 f eet below t he existing ground  s urface at t he app roximate 
location s hown on t he Sit e Pl an, Plat e 2.  T he load test s
haft locat ion ( LT-A) is  at  
approximately Sta. 503+70 of the HHCTCP fixed-guideway alignment as indicated on the 
Site Plan (Plate 2). The ground surface elevation at the load test shaft location (LT-A) is at 
approximately +154 feet MSL. 
The load t est shaft was stru cturally reinforced and instrumented with six levels of 
embedment strai n gages for l oad testing purposes . As a mi nimum, embedment strain 
gages were provided at each level with additional strain gages at selected levels where we 
believe would provide for some additional information. In addi tion, some thermistors also 
were installed to  supplement the concrete  temperature me asurements in the drilled shaft 
concrete from the embedment strain gages for mass concrete considerations. 
During the load test  shaft installation for LT-A, a 
Geolabs engineer observed the 
materials encountered duri ng the shaft excavati on, and a l og of the subsurface materials 
encountered was maintained. In addition, the Geol abs engineer also maint ained a log  of 
the concrete volume  placed in the load te st shaft as a functi on of depth throughout th e 
concrete placement. The log mai ntained by the Geol
abs engineer is presented in 
Appendix C. 
4.4 
Miniature Shaft Inspection Device 
To engage the end bearing component of the drilled shaft, the base of the drilled  
shaft excavation will need to be relatively
 clean and free of loos
e sediments before 
LT-A  11/19/2009
SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. Page 
12 
Hawaii ● California 
concrete placement. To check the base of the drilled shaft before concrete placemen t 
and to provide a higher degree of confidence
 that the drilled s haft base is relatively 
clean and free of loose sediment
s, we utilized the miniature Sh
aft Inspection Devic e 
(Mini-SID) to check the base of the drill
ed shaft for cleanliness when using the 
end-bearing component for foundation support.  
The Mini-SID was manufactured by GPE, Inc. of Gaine sville, Florida. The device 
consists of a waterproof encased color video camera mounted in an inspection bell. The 
inspection bell is lowe red to the bottom of the drilled s haft with the aid of an electrical 
motor hoist. Once the  inspection bell is s eated on the bottom of t he drilled shaft, live 
video is transmitted to a video control 
unit where the Engineer, Inspector, and 
Contractor can view and evaluate the shaft bottom at the same ti me. The drilled shaft is 
considered acceptable for end- bearing support if 50% or more of the shaft bottom 
contains 0.5 inches or  less of sediments. If any part of the shaft bottom contains more 
than 1.5 inches of sediment
s, the drille d shaft is considere
d unaccep table. Th e 
methodology of the visual inspection and the 
results from the Mini-SID per formed on 
Load Test Shaft LT-A are presented in Appendix D. 
4.5 
Concrete Tests 
Temperature control of mass  concrete is necessary to prevent cracking caused 
by excessive tensile strains that result fr
om differential co oling of the concrete. The 
concrete is heated by  reaction of cement with water and can gain additional heat from 
exposure to the ambient conditions. Cracki ng may be controlled by methods that limi
t 
the peak temperature to a safe  level, so the tensile stra ins developed as the concrete 
cools to equilibrium are less than the tensile  strain capacity. For the HHCTCP project, 
we unders tand the maximum temperature of
 the concrete has been s
pecified at  
160 degrees Fahrenheit or less and the maximu m differential between the center and 
the edge of the concrete mass is specified at 35 degrees Fahrenheit or less. 
Temperature measurements of the concre te placed in the Load Test Shaft LT-A 
were recorded for about 16 days since concrete placement, and the measured 
temperatures are presented in Appendix E. In addition, concrete temperature, slump, air 
content, and unit weight measurements of the fresh concrete are summarized in

Hawaii ● California 
Appendix E. The unconfined compression tests on the concrete cylinders taken also are 
summarized in Appendix E. 
4.6 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL) testing was performed to evaluate the integrity of 
the concrete drilled shaft. The CSL testing was conducted using the CHUM CSL Testing 
equipment manufactured by Piletest. CS
L tests were performed between all the 
possible access tube combinations in the load te st shaft (8 tubes in the load test shaft 
resulting in 28 profiles). 
Eight steel access tubes were installed in the load test shaft. Generally, the steel  
access tubes were extended approximately  9 feet above the top of the shaft concrete 
for access considerations. Potable water 
was used to fill the access tubes followin
g 
concrete placement, and the Contractor monitored the water level in the access tubes to 
maintain the water level in the access tubes until the CSL testing. The methodology and 
results of the CSL tests are presented in Appendix F. 
4.7 
Osterberg Load Test 
Due to the hi gh capacities anticipated for the drill ed shafts, conventional load tests 
would not be practi cal and would be costly to conduct. Therefore, bi-directional axial load 
test using expandabl e load cells (Osterberg Load C ells) was adopted for the drill ed shaft 
load test program. The bi- directional load test separately tests the shear resistance and 
end-bearing components of the drilled shaft by loading the shaft in two directions (upward 
for shear resistance, and downward for end-bearing and shear resistance). 
The Osterberg Load Cells was capable of applying a load of at least 6,000 kips in 
each direction. The expandable load cell was attached to the reinforcing steel cage prior 
to lowering the cage into the drilled hole. 
The drilled s haft load test was performed in 
general accordance with the Quick Load Test Method of ASTM D 1143. The load test 
shaft was loaded to failure to evaluate the ultimate side shear resistance and 
end-bearing components of the load test shaft.  
Installation of the expandable load cells, 
installation of t he embedment strain 
gages, performance of the bi-directional axia l load tests, and pres entation of the load

c. The load test report prepared by

SECTION 5.  LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this report are based 
upon information obtained from the field explorat
ion points. Variations of subsurface 
conditions between and beyond the exploration point s may occur, and the nature and 
extent of these variations may not become 
evident until additional field investigation 
and/or construction is underway. 
The locations of the field exploration points (B-1 and B-1A) indicated in this report 
have been staked out by R.M. To will Corporation. The elevations of B-1 and B-1A were 
interpolated between the spot elevat
ions shown on the topographic survey. The 
physical locations and elevatio ns of the field exploration points should be considered 
accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratifi cation lines shown on the graphic representations
 of the fi
eld 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Wa
ter level data presented on the logs of
borings in this report were based on measurem ents recorded in the borings at the times  
shown on the graphic  representations. It shoul d be noted that fl uctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the excl
usive use of the City and County of 
Honolulu, Department of Tran
sportation Services, for specific applic
ation to the 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Pr oject in accordance wit h generally accepted 
geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This report is limited in scope and is for the project design advancement and cost 
estimation purposes only. The borings for this
 drilled shaft load test program did not 
encounter cavities or underground structures
, such as obstructions, buried debris,
storage tanks, etc., that may be present at the project site.  
This repor t was not intended to investigate the potential f
or presence of
hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should  be noted that the equipment,

a geo-environmental exp loration differ

Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions at the location of  the drilled shaft load test , 
designated as LT-A, by drilling and sampling on e boring, designated as Boring No. 1. The 
boring extended to a dept h of approximately 86.5 feet below the existing ground surface 
using a Mobile B-80 truck-moun
ted dri ll rig equipped wi th co ntinuous-flight augers. In 
addition, a second boring (Borin g No. 1A) was drilled at  about 35 feet to  the southwest of 
Boring No. 1 to evaluate the consistency (thickness and composition) of the granular layer 
encountered at about 10 to 15 
feet in depth i n Boring No. 1. The fi eld exploration was 
conducted on July 30,  July 31 and August 1, 2009. The approximate boring locations are 
shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2. 
Generally, the s oil bor ings wer e adv anced with 4- inch diam eter cont inuous-flight 
solid-stem augers. Aft er the borings wer e adv anced t o the max imum dept h of t he 
borehole, the bored hol es generally were backfilled with bentonite chips starting from the 
bottom of the hole and hydrated. 
Our g eologist cla ssified the material s enc ountered in t he bor ings by visual  and 
textural exam ination in t he f ield in gene ral a ccordance wi th AST M D  2488, S tandard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on 
a near -continuous basis. T hese c lassifications we re fu rther re viewed visuall y and by 
testing in the labo ratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D 2487, 
Standard Pr actice for Clas sification of Soil s for Engineer ing Purpos es ( Unified Soil 
Classification S ystem), as shown on t
he S oil Log Legend (Pl
ate A ). Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered in the two borings are presented on the Logs 
of Borings, Plates A-1 and A-2. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil  sampl es w ere obtained from th e drilled bori ngs i n 
general accordance wi th AS TM D 3550, S tandard P ractice for T hick W all, Ring- Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by dr iving a 3- inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from 
the drilled borings in gener al accordance wi th ASTM D  1586, Standard Test Method for 
Penetration Test and Spl it-Barrel Sam pling of Soils, by dr
iving a 2-inch O D st andard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sam pler the s econd and t hird 6 inches of an 18-inch dr
ive are shown as t
he 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Pocket pen etrometer tes ts we re p erformed o n sele cted coh esive soil  sampl es 
retrieved in t
he f ield. T he poc ket penetr ometer t est pr ovides an indi cation of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the s oil sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are 
presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.

A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exp
loration performed for the Drilled Shaft 
Load Test Program for LT-A were visually ex amined and classified in our laboratory in 
Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were tested to evaluate the physical characteristics 
and engineering properties of the soils encount
ered. The laboratory testi ng program 
was formulated with the emphasis on evaluating 
the moisture, density, plasticity, and 
undrained shear strength of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included det ermination of dry density and natural 
moisture content, Atterberg Limits, and triaxi al compression tests. Results of the tests  
performed are presented on Plat
es B-1 through B-14. Detailed descriptions of the 
various tests performed for this exploration are presented below. 
Moisture content and dry density tests were performed on selected soil samples  
to aid in defining the inte
rface between the various so
il layers encountered. The 
moisture content tests were performed in  general ac cordance with ASTM  D 2216, and 
the dry unit weight (dry density) test wa s conducted in accordanc e with ASTM D 7263. 
The test results are presented on the Logs of Boring at the appropriate sample depths. 
Five (5) Atterberg Limits tests were
 performed on selected soil samples to 
evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (P
L), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid i
n 
classifying the soils. The Atterberg Limits tests were performed i n general accordanc e 
with ASTM D 4318. T he test results are su mmarized on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths, and gr aphic presentation of the te st results is provided on 
Plate B-1. 
To evaluate the undrained shear strengt
h of the clayey soils,
 thirteen (13)  
Unconsolidated Undr ained Tria xial Compression tests were performed in accordanc
e 
with AST M D 2850 on selected soil samples.
 The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden stress was used as the applied c
onfining pressure for the relatively
“undisturbed” soil samples. The test result s and the stress-strain curves are presented 
on Plates B-2 through B-14.

Load Test Shaft Installation 
The load test shaft was ex
cavated using a Bauer B G-40 drill  rig equipped wi th 
96-inch diameter digging bucket, co re barrel, and clean-out bucke t. The actual location of 
the load test shaft is shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2. 
Because groundwater was not encountered during the dr illing process, no drilling 
slurry was utilized during excava tion for the load test sha ft. However, a temporary stee l 
casing was used during excavation of t he test shaft to avoid the potential for caving-in of 
the surface soil s at th e top of the shaft and to  provide for fa ll protection to ward the open 
excavation. Th e top  o f th e te mporary steel  casing ex tended about 5 feet above the  
existing ground elevat ion. The outside diameter (O D) of the temporary steel casing was 
103 inches with a c asing thickness of 0.75 inches. The bottom of t he casing extended to 
about 10 feet below the ground surface. 
After excavation of Load 
Test Sha ft LT -A to i ts sp ecified tip elevation, the 
reinforcing steel c age was lower ed int o t he dr illed hole to the pre-determined elevation 
using a Link-Belt 8990 mobile crane. Reinforcing steel cage spacers were used to provide 
the c learance as sociated wit h a m inimum co ncrete cover of 3 inc
hes between t he 
sidewalls of the drilled hole and the sides of the reinforcing cage. After the reinforcing cage 
was installed in the drilled hole, concrete was placed through a 4-inch ID steel tremie pipe 
starting from the bottom. 
During the load test  shaft installation for LT-A, a 
Geolabs engineer observed the 
materials encountered duri ng the shaft excavati on, and a l og of the subsurface materials 
encountered was maintained. In addition, the Geol abs engineer also maint ained a log o f 
the concrete volume placed in t he load test shaft as a function of dept h during concrete 
placement. The dr illed shaft installation 
log maintained by the 
Geolabs engineer is 
presented as Plates C-1 through C-4.

Miniature Shaft Inspection Device 
To engage the end b earing component of the drilled shaft, we recommend using 
a miniature Shaft Inspection Device (Mini-SID) to check the base of the drilled shafts for 
cleanliness when using the end-bearing component for foundation support.  
The Mini-SID consists of a waterproof encased color video camera mounted in 
an inspection bell. The inspecti on bell is comprised of a we ighted stainless steel skirt 
equipped with LED lights, two water jets, a compressed air inlet, two pass/fail indicators, 
and a depth of debris indicator. The two pass/fa
il indicators are marked at 0.5 inches 
and 1.5 inches. The depth of debris indic
ator is marked at 0.5-inch increments to a
maximum of 3 inches. A three-hose umbilical cord used to transport air, water, electrical 
and video cables connect to the top of the inspection bell. 
The inspection bell is lowered to the botto m of the drilled shaft with the aid of  an 
electrical hoist. Once on the bottom, live video 
is transmitted to a video control unit at 
the ground surface where the Engineer, In
spector, and Cont ractor can view and 
evaluate the shaft bottom at the same time. The live vi deo can be recorded and stored 
to a DVD for later evaluation.  
The inspection is performed at fi ve locations on the bottom of the shaft. The five 
locations include the center, north, east, south, and west sides of the shaft. The shaft is  
considered acceptable if 50% or more of th e shaft bottom contains less than 0.5 inches  
of sediment. If any part of t he shaft bottom contains more than 1.5 inches of sediment, 
the shaft is considered unacceptable.  
The results of the Mini-SID performed on
 Load Test Shaft LT-A show that all 
views pas sed with approximatel
y 74% of the shaft bottom containing less than 
0.5 inches of sediment. Results from t
he Mini-SID inspection performed on LT-A are 
presented on Plates D-1 through D-6.

A P P E N D I X   E 
Concrete Tests 
Mass concrete is defined by the Americ
an Concrete Institute (A CI) as follows: 
Any volum e of concrete with dimensions 
large enough to require that measures be 
taken to cope with generation 
of heat from hydration of 
the cement and attendant 
volume change to minimize cracking. As a 
general rule of thumb,  any placement of 
structural concrete with a minimum dim
ension equal to or 
greater than 1 meter 
(39 inches) should be considered mass concre te. Similar consideration should be given 
to other concrete placements that do not 
meet this minimum dimension but contain 
ASTM C150 Type III or ASTM C1157 HE 
cement, accelerating admixtures, or 
cementitious materials in excess of 600 lbs/yd3 of concrete. 
All concret e generate heat as the cement
itious materials hydrate. Heat is a 
byproduct of the hydration reactions, which gi ves concrete its strength and durability. I n 
most placements, the heat escapes almost as
 rapidly  as it is generated. In a mass 
concrete placement, the heat escapes more sl
owly than it is generated. The result is 
that temperatures within the concrete can get quite hot and high enough to damage the 
concrete. If the internal temperature 
exceeds 160 degrees Fahrenheit, the long term 
durability of some concretes may be affected by delayed ettringite formation (DEF). 
DEF is rare, and only certain concretes may be affected. When DEF occurs, the 
concrete paste expands and cracks the concrete with detrimental results, which may not 
be evident for many years. Additionally, while the interior of the concrete mass can be 
quite hot, the surface can be relatively cool . The resulting large temperature difference 
results in large thermal stresses, which can cause cracking of t he surface. Historically, 
limiting the temperature difference between the 
interior and surface so that it is less 
than 35 degrees Fahrenheit has been found to pr
event or minimize thermal cracking. 
Certain concretes are more tolerant of
 thermal cracking than others, and these 
concretes can withstand a higher temperature difference without thermally cracking. 
To prevent high internal temperatures
 and large temperature differences, an 
appropriate mix desig n should b e selected. This will reduce other efforts to control 
temperatures and temperature differences after placement. The temperature rise of 
concrete is directly related to the types and quantities of cementitious materials in the 
concrete. An appropriate mi
x design contains the leas
t amount of cementitious
materials needed for strength and durability . Placeability of concrete also should factor 
into the concrete mix des
ign. This some times increases the cementitious cement 
content. To reduce heat of hydr ation, Class F fly ash or slag cement typically is used t o 
replace a portion of the cement. The perc entage depends on several factors including 
environmental exposure and durability requirements. 
LT-A  11/19/2009
Appendix E 
Concrete Tests 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page E-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Research suggests that the cracking of
 mass concrete may be lowered by a 
variety of methods, including the following: 
Reduction of the fresh concrete temperature 
Use of a larger maximum size aggregate 
Use of aggregate with a low coefficient of thermal expansion 
Use of crushed aggregate instead of smooth, round aggregate 
Replacement of cement with fl
y as h, slag, or suitable supplementary 
cementitious materials (SCMs) 
 Entrained 
air 
Reduction of cement content and paste content 
To document the means and methods t
hat are required and  will be us ed, a 
thermal control plan will need to be developed for the project. A thermal control plan will 
allow all involved parties to agree on the measures that will be used, and the expected 
results. Such measures may include precooling of the concrete, cooling pipe installation 
and operation, insulation, temperature monitoring equipment and locations.  
For the load test shaft, some measures were taken in an attempt to reduce the 
heat generated in the drilled shaft concrete. However, it should be noted that we did no t 
take extraordinary measures to keep the 
maximum concrete temperatures and the 
maximum temperature differentials to the specified levels for the project. In general, we 
used an air-entrained concrete to reduce the 
amount of cement in the concrete in an 
effort to reduce the overall heat generated from the hydrating concrete.  
The drilled  shaft concrete mix utilize d for the load test shaft was supplied by 
Island Ready Mix Concrete, Inc. (Mix No
. 4569HVMS). The concrete temperature, 
slump, air content, and unit we ight of the fresh concrete measured in the fi
eld during 
concrete placement are presented on Plate E-1. Results of the unconfined compression 
test results on the concrete cylinders at
 various cure days (age) are presented on 
Plate E-2. Results of the te
mperature measurements in t he drilled shaft concrete at 
various depths for a period of about 16 days (384 hours) are presented on Plates E-3 
through E-9.

A P P E N D I X   F 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
CSL testing was performed to evaluate the integrity of the c oncrete drilled shaft 
for the portion of the load test shaft above the load cell only. To ensure that a fracture 
plane is created at the base of  the load cell, steel CSL t ubes were not extended below  
the top of the load cell plate. 
Generally, CSL tests measure the travel 
time of an ultrasonic pulse wav
e to 
travel from a signal source in one access tube to a receiver in another access tube. The 
test is performed by lowering the sign
al and receiver probe to the bottom of 
two separate access tubes. The transmitter and receiver probes are then pulled upwar d 
simultaneously so they are a lways located at the same hei ght. While being pulled, the 
probes are continuously sending and receiving ul trasonic pulse waves. The first arrival 
time of the ultrasonic pulse wave is recorded every 2 inches (50 mm). 
The CSL test was conducted using 
the CHUM CSL Te
sting equipment 
manufactured by Pilet est. CSL tests were  performed between all the possible access
tube combinations in the load test shaft (8 t ubes in the shaft result ing in 28 profiles per 
shaft). The northern most access tube was designated as Tube 1. 
Steel access tubes were installed in t he load test shaft above the load c
ell top 
plate only. Generally, the steel access tubes were extended approximately 9 feet above 
the top of the shaft concrete for access c
onsiderations. Please not e that the top of 
concrete is approximately 5 feet below the ground surface. Potable water was used to 
fill the access tubes following c
oncrete plac ement, and the Contra ctor monitored the 
water level in the access tubes to keep the water level in the access tubes until the CSL 
tests. 
Based on the results of the CSL tests, 
the increased f irst arrival times (FATs) 
were less than 12% typically, and the wave 
energy was relatively uniform in all the 
profiles within the depths of the drilled shaft tested. 
As a general guideline, increases in 
FATs of 10% or less may be conside
red 
insignificant. Increases in FATs between 10% and 25% usually are considered minor, 
but verified defects have been discovered in 
some cases, while increases in FATs 
greater than 25% usually indicate anomalie
s worthy of consideration and further 
evaluation. Changes in relative energy can 
sometimes indicate defects depending on  
the degree of the reduction and the associated increase in FAT. 
The average apparent ultrasonic pulse wave velocity can be determ ined from the 
CSL test resul ts by di viding the average tube  distances by t he average first arrival times 
for each tube combinati on in  a shaft. As shown on Plate 
F-1, the aver age apparent  
ultrasonic pulse wave velocity for the shaft was 12,680 feet per second. It should be noted 
that the average apparen t ultrasonic pulse wave veloci ty computed in this manner i s not 
LT-A  11/19/2009
Appendix F 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page F-2 
Hawaii ● California 
an absolute number and should be used wi th caution because the actual tube distances 
below the top of the shaft are not known accurately. 
Based on the above di scussion and ou r field observation, we believe Load Test 
Shaft LT-A was install
ed pr operly meeting the requi
rements of typi
cal plans and 
specifications.

The apparent sonic wave velocity is an indication of the overall quality of the concrete and was estimated by 
dividing the average tube distance by the average first arrival time for all tube combinations.  Sonic wave 
velocities for cast-in-place concrete generally range from 10,000 to 13,000 feet per second.

A P P E N D I X   G 
Osterberg Load Test 
An Osterberg load cell test was conducted on the load test shaft (designated as 
LT-A) by LOADTEST,  Inc. on August 19, 2009. The load test shaft was excavated by 
CMZ of Hawaii, Inc. under the 
observation of representati ves from Geolabs , Inc. The 
load cell assembly and test shaft instrument ation were carried out under the direction of  
Mr. Denton A. Kort of LOADTEST, Inc. 
Detailed discussions, data, and analys is of the O-cell load test are presented in 
the report entitled “Report on 
Drilled Shaft Load Tes ting (Osterberg Method), LT-A , 
Honolulu HCTC, Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1)
” dated August 26, 2009 prepared by 
LOADTEST, Inc. A copy of the report is incl uded in this appendix for ease of reference. 
The following table provides a summary of the load test results.

REPORT ON DRILLED SHAFT 
LOAD TESTING (OSTERBERG METHOD) 
Pile Shape by SONICALIPER 
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1)

LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 
On August 19, 2009, we tested a nominal 96-inch (2,438-mm) diameter dedicated 
test shaft constructed by CMZ of Hawaii.  Mr. Denton A. Kort of LOADTEST, Inc. 
carried out the test.  CMZ of Hawaii completed construction of the 60.7-foot (18.49-
meter) deep shaft in the dry on August 12, 2009.  Sub-surface conditions at the test 
shaft location consist primarily of stiff to hard clayey silt and silty clay.  
Representatives of Geolabs, Inc. and others observed construction and testing of 
the shaft. 
The maximum sustained bi-directional load applied to the shaft was 5,067 kips 
(22.54 MN).  At the maximum load, the displacements above and below the O-cell 
were 0.237 inches (6.01 mm) and 6.000 inches (152.40 mm), respectively.  Unit 
shear data calculated from strain gages indicated an average net unit side shear of 
4.0 ksf (191 kPa) and 8.1 ksf (390 kPa) above and below the O-cell, respectively, at 
the above noted respective displacements. The maximum applied unit end bearing 
is calculated to be 55.3 ksf (2,647 kPa).   
Using the procedures described in the report text and in Appendix C, we constructed 
an equivalent top load curve for the test shaft.  For a top loading of 6,616 kips (29.4 
MN), the adjusted test data indicate this shaft would settle approximately 0.25 inches 
(6.4 mm) of which 0.07 inches (1.8 mm) is estimated elastic compression. 
A ultrasonic caliper (SONICALIPER) was used to generate profiles of the shaft 
excavation sidewalls prior to installation of the O-cell assembly.  A summary of the 
caliper data and the dimensions used in the analyses are included in Appendix H. 
LIMITATIONS OF EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 
We include this executive summary to provide a very brief presentation of some of 
the key elements of this O-cell test.  It is by no means intended to be a 
comprehensive or stand-alone representation of the test results.  The full text of the 
report and the attached appendices contain important information which the 
engineer can use to come to more informed conclusions about the data presented 
herein.   
LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1)

SITE CONDITIONS AND SHAFT CONSTRUCTION 
Site Sub-surface Conditions:  The sub-surface stratigraphy at the general location 
of the test shaft is reported to consist of stiff to hard clayey silt and silty clay.  The 
generalized subsurface profile is included in Figure A and a boring log indicating 
conditions near the shaft is presented in Appendix E. 
Test Shaft Construction:  CMZ of Hawaii began excavation of the dedicated test 
shaft on August 11, 2009 and performed the final cleanout and concreting on August 
12, 2009.  We understand that the nominal 96-inch (2,438-mm) test shaft was 
excavated to a tip elevation of +93.4 feet (+28.45 meters) in the dry.  The shaft was 
started by installing a 103-inch (2,616-mm) O.D. temporary surface casing using a 
rig-mounted casing rotator.  A digging bucket was used for excavating the remainder 
of the shaft.  The bottom of the shaft was cleaned with a cleanout bucket after 
drilling and the SONICALIPER was then used to profile the shaft excavation.  After 
a final cleaning of the base, the reinforcing cage with attached O-cell assembly was 
inserted into the excavation and temporarily supported from the casing.  Concrete 
was then delivered by pump through a 4-inch (102-mm) O.D. pipe into the base of 
the shaft until the top of the concrete reached an elevation of +148.8 feet (+45.34 
meters).  The contractor removed the 103-inch (2,616-mm) O.D. casing immediately 
after concrete placement.  Representatives of Geolabs, Inc. and others observed 
construction of the shaft.  
OSTERBERG CELL TESTING 
Shaft Instrumentation:  Test shaft instrumentation and assembly was carried out 
under the direction of Denton A. Kort of LOADTEST, Inc. between August 7, 2009 
and August 10, 2009.  The loading assembly consisted of one 34-inch (860-mm) O-
cell located 10.2 feet (3.09 meters) above the tip of shaft.  Calibrations of the O-cell 
and instrumentation used for this test are included in Appendix B.  
O-cell testing instrumentation included four Linear Vibrating Wire Displacement 
Transducers (LVWDTs - Geokon Model 4450 series) positioned between the lower 
and upper plates of the O-cell assembly to measure expansion. Two shaft tip telltale 
casings (nominal ½-inch steel pipe) were also attached to the reinforcing cage, 
diametrically opposed, extending from the tip of the rebar cage to beyond the top of 
concrete to measure tip displacement.  The shaft tip telltale casings were 
discontinuous at the O-cell location to enable venting of the fracture plane in the 
shaft formed by the expansion of the O-cell.   
Two telltale casings (nominal ½-inch steel pipe) were attached to the reinforcing 
cage, diametrically opposed, extending from the top of the O-cell assembly to 
beyond the top of concrete to measure compression above the O-cell.   
LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 2 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
Strain gages were used to assess the side shear load transfer of the shaft above 
and below the Osterberg cell assembly.  Five levels of two diametrically-opposed 
vibrating wire embedment strain gages (Geokon Model 4200 series) were installed 
in the shaft above the base of the O-cell assembly and two levels of two were 
installed in the shaft below it.  Additional gages were installed at strain gage level 
one (total of 5 gages) and at strain gage levels 2 and 3 (total of 3 gages).  Two 
vibrating wire earth pressure cells (Geokon Model 4810) were installed near the tip 
of the rebar cage to measure applied end bearing pressure.  Details concerning the 
instrumentation position appear in Table B and Figures A & B.  Data collected from 
these instruments are included in Appendix A. 
Test Arrangement:  Throughout the load test, key elements of shaft response were 
monitored using the equipment and instruments described herein.  Telltales were 
installed in the nominal ½-inch steel pipes (described under Shaft Instrumentation) 
and monitored by Linear Variable Displacement Transducers (LVDTs - RDP Series).  
Two automated digital survey levels (Leica NA 3000 Series) were used to monitor 
the top of shaft movement from a distance of approximately 41 feet (12.6 meters). 
Data collected from these instruments are included in Appendix A, Pages 1 - 2).   
A Bourdon pressure gage and vibrating wire and electronic pressure transducers 
were used to measure the pressure applied to the O-cell at each load interval.  We 
used the transducers for automatically setting and maintaining loads and for data 
analysis and real time plotting.  The Bourdon gage was used as a check on the 
transducers.  There was close agreement between the Bourdon gage and the 
pressure transducers.   
Data Acquisition:  All instrumentation were connected through a data logger 
(Campbell Scientific CR-10) to a laptop computer allowing data to be recorded and 
stored automatically at 60-second intervals and displayed in real time.  The same 
laptop computer synchronized to the data logging system was used to acquire the 
Leica NA3000 data sets.  
Testing Procedures:  As with all of our tests, we begin by pressurizing the O-cell in 
order to break the tack welds that hold it closed (for handling and for placement in 
the shaft) and to form the fracture plane in the concrete surrounding the base of the 
O-cell.  After the break occurs, we immediately release the pressure and then begin 
the loading procedure.  Zero readings for all instrumentation are taken prior to the 
preliminary weld-breaking load-unload cycle, which in this case involved a maximum 
applied load of 1,573 kips (7.0 MN).  
The Osterberg cell load test was conducted as follows: We pressurized the 34-inch 
(860-mm) diameter O-cell, with its base located 10.2 feet (3.09 meters) above the 
base of shaft to assess the combined end bearing and lower side shear below the 
O-cell and the upper side shear above.  We loaded the shaft in 14 loading 
increments to a bi-directional gross O-cell load of 4,223 kips (18.79 MN).  The 
loading was halted and the O-cell pressure allowed to bleed-off while awaiting 
LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 3 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
delivery of additional hydraulic fluid.  Loading continued in three increments to 5,067 
kips (22.54 MN).  After the four-minute reading of increment 2L-3, the O-cell 
exceeded its nominal stroke and the shaft was unloaded in one decrement, 
concluding the test.   
We carried out the test using Procedure A: Quick Test loading schedule (ASTM 
D1143-07 Standard Test Methods for Deep Foundations Under Static Axial 
Compressive Load), holding each successive load increment constant for eight 
minutes (Appendix A, Figure 2).  The data logger automatically recorded the 
instrument readings every 60 seconds, but herein we report only the 1, 2, 4 and 8-
minute readings (where applicable) during each increment of maintained load.  
Additional readings are reported during extended holds at increments 1L-10, 1L-14 
and 1U-1. 
TEST RESULTS AND ANALYSES 
General:  The loads applied by the O-cell act in two opposing directions, resisted by 
the capacity of the shaft above and below.  Theoretically, the O-cell does not impose 
an additional upward load until its expansion force exceeds the buoyant weight of 
the shaft above the O-cell.  Therefore, net load, which is defined as gross O-cell load 
minus the buoyant weight of the shaft above, is used to determine side shear 
resistance above the O-cell and to construct the equivalent top-loaded load-
settlement curve.  For this test we calculated a buoyant weight of shaft of 374 kips 
(1.66 MN) above the O-cell.   
Pile Shape and Dimensions:  SONICALIPER profiles were taken of the 
excavation to determine the geometry of the shaft.  In the analyses, we use the 
nominal shaft dimensions between elevations +148.75 feet and +142.75 feet, and 
those obtained from the ultrasonic caliper profile between elevations +142.75 feet 
and +93.75 feet.  A summary of the ultrasonic caliper data, the profiles and the 
dimensions used in the analyses are included in Appendix H.  The results indicate 
relatively uniform cross section and only minimal verticality deviation.  The Engineer 
should consider the effect of shaft shape on shear resistance when evaluating the 
test results. 
Upper Side Shear Resistance:  The maximum upward applied net load to the 
upper side shear was 4,693 kips (20.87 MN) which occurred at the four minute 
reading of load interval 2L-3.  At this loading, the upward movement of the O-cell top 
plate was 0.237 inches (6.01 mm) (Appendix A, Page 6, Figures 1 - 3).   
In order to assess the side shear resistance of the test shaft, loads are calculated 
based on the strain gage data (Appendix A, Pages 7 through 14, Figures 4 and 5) 
and estimates of shaft stiffness (AE) which are presented below and in Appendix G.  
LT-A  11/19/2009
LT-A - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 4 
Ewa, Hawaii (LT-9581-1)

O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
We used the ACI formula (Ec=57000√f´c) to calculate an elastic modulus for the 
concrete, where f’c was reported to be 6,150 psi (42.40 MPa) on the day of the test.  
This, combined with the area of reinforcing steel and nominal and measured shaft 
diameters, provided an average shaft stiffness (AE) of 37,900,000 kips (169,000 
MN) above the O-cell in the temporary cased section, 35,100,000 kips (156,000 MN) 
above the O-cell in the uncased section and 34,600,000 kips (154,000 MN) below 
the O-cell.  We estimate a strain-dependent stiffness for the shaft section below the 
O-cell based on the analysis presented in Appendix G.  Mobilized net unit shear 
curves are presented in Figures 6a and 6b and tabulated displacements and net unit 
shear values are presented in Appendix I.  Net unit shear values for loading 
increment 2L-3 follow in Table A:

Combined End Bearing and Lower Side Shear Resistance:  The maximum O-cell 
load applied to the combined end bearing and lower side shear was 5,067 kips 
(18.79 MN) which occurred at the four-minute reading of load interval 2L-3 
(Appendix A, Page 6, Figures 1 - 3).  At this loading, the average downward 
movement of the O-cell base was 6.000 inches (152.40 mm).  The load taken in 
shear by the 10.2 feet (3.09 meters) of shaft section below the O-cell is calculated to 
be 2,130 kips (9.47 MN) based on the load distribution in Figure 5, and the 
measured shaft diameter.  The applied load to end bearing is then 2,937 kips (13.06 
MN) and the unit end bearing at the base of the shaft is calculated to be 55.3 ksf 
(2,647 kPa) at the above noted displacement.  Applied end bearing pressure was 
also measured using two earth pressure cells installed near the tip of the shaft.  The 
pressure cells indicated a maximum applied end bearing pressure of 50 ksf (2,417 
kPa) at the above noted maximum displacement. Unit end bearing curves are 
presented in Figure 7.

Equivalent Top Load:  Figure 8 presents the equivalent top-loaded load-settlement 
curves.  The lighter curve, described in Procedure Part I of Appendix C, was 
generated by using the measured upward top of O-cell and downward base of O-cell 
data.  The curve is extended out to a settlement of 0.50 inches (13 mm) by 
extrapolating the top of O-cell data (Appendix F).  Because it is often an important 
component of the settlements involved, the equivalent top load curve requires an 
adjustment for the additional elastic compression that would occur in a top-load test.  
The darker curve as described in Procedure Part II of Appendix C includes this 
adjustment.   
We mobilized a combined side shear and end bearing resistance of 8,916 kips (39.7 
MN) during the test.  For an equivalent top loading of 6,616 kips (29.4 MN), the 
adjusted test data indicate this shaft would settle approximately 0.25 inches (6.4 
mm) of which 0.07 inches (1.8 mm) is estimated elastic compression.  For an 
equivalent top loading of 7,602 kips (33.8 MN) the adjusted test data indicate this 
shaft would settle approximately 0.50 inches (12.7 mm) of which 0.08 inches (2.1 
mm) is estimated elastic compression.   
Note that, as explained previously, the equivalent top load curve applies to 
incremental loading durations of eight minutes.  Creep effects will reduce the 
ultimate resistance of both components and increase shaft top movement for a given 
loading over longer times.  The Engineer can estimate such additional creep effects 
by suitable extrapolation of time effects using the creep data presented herein.   
Creep Limit:  See Appendix D for our O-cell method for determining creep limit.  
The combined end bearing and lower side shear creep data (Appendix A, Pages 5 
and 6) indicate that a creep limit of 2,115 kips (9.4 MN) was reached at a downward 
displacement of 0.11 inches (2.8 mm) (Appendix D, Figure 1).  The upper side shear 
creep data (Appendix A, Pages 5 and 6) indicate that no apparent creep limit was 
reached at the maximum upward displacement of 0.24 inches (6.0 mm) (Appendix 
D, Figure 2).  The creep limit for a top-loaded shaft will not be reached until both 
shaft components reach their respective creep limits.  The engineer should come to 
his own conclusions with regard to the suitability of the creep limit analysis to 
address long term creep which may be an important design consideration.

APPENDIX D 
O-CELL METHOD FOR DETERMINING 
CREEP LIMIT LOADING 
LT-A  11/19/2009
O-CELL METHOD FOR DETERMINING A CREEP LIMIT LOADING 
ON THE EQUIVALENT TOP-LOADED SHAFT (September, 2000) 
Background:  O-cell testing provides a sometimes useful method for evaluating 
that load beyond which a top-loaded drilled 
shaft might experience significant 
unwanted creep behavior.  We refer to th
is load as the “creep limit,” also 
sometimes known as the “yield limit” or “yield load”. 
To our knowledge, Housel (1959) firs t proposed the method described below for 
determining the creep limit.  Stoll 
(1961), Bourges and Levillian (1988), and 
Fellenius (1996) provide additional referenc es.  This method also follows from 
long experience with the pressuremeter te
st (PMT).  Figure 8 and section 9.4 
from ASTM D4719-94, reproduced below, 
show and describe the creep curve 
routinely determined from the PMT.  T he creep curve shows how the movement 
or strain obtained over a fixed time in terval, 30 to 60 seconds, changes versus 
the applied pressure.  One can often detect 
a distinct break in the curve at the 
pressure P e in Figure 8.  Plastic deformati
ons may become significant beyond 
this break loading and progressively more severe creep can occur. 
Definition: Similarly with O-cell testing using the ASTM Quick Method, one can 
conveniently measure the additional move
ment occurring over the final time 
interval at each constant load step, typically 2 to 4 minutes.  A break in the curve 
of load vs. movement (as at Pe with the PMT) indicates the creep limit. 
We usually indicate such a creep limit in t he O-cell test for either one, or both, of 
the side shear and end bearing component
s, and herein designate the 
corresponding  movements as M CL1 and M CL2.  We then combine the creep limit 
data to predict a creep limit load for the equivalent top loaded shaft. 
Procedure if both MCL1 and MCL2 available:   Creep cannot begin until the shaft 
movement exceeds the MCL values.  A conservative approach would assume that 
creep begins when movements exceed the lesser of the M
CL values.  However, 
creep can occur freely only when the shaft has moved the greater of the two M CL 
values.  Although less conservative, we 
believe the latter to match behavior 
better and therefore set the creep limit as  that load on the equivalent top-loaded 
movement curve that matches the greater MCL. 
Procedure if only MCL1 available:  If we cannot determine a creep limit in the 
second component before it reac hes its maximum movement M x, we treat M x as 
MCL2.  From the above method one can say t hat the creep limit load exceeds, by 
some unknown amount, that obtained when using MCL2 = Mx. 
LT-A  11/19/2009
Procedure if no creep limit observed: Then, according to the above, the creep 
limit for the equivalent top-loaded sha
ft will exceed, again by some unknown 
amount, that load on the equivalent curve 
that matches the movement of the 
component with the maximum movement. 
Limitations:  The accuracy in estimating creep limits depends, in part, on the 
scatter of the data in the creep limit plots.  The more scatte r, the more difficult to 
define a limit.  The user should make his 
or her own interpretation if he or she 
intends to make important use of the creep limit interpretations.  Sometimes we 
obtain excessive scatter of the data and 
do not attempt an interpretation for a 
creep limit and will indicate this in the report. 
Excerpts from ASTM D4719 
“Standard Test Method for Pressuremeter Testing in Soils” 
9.4 For Procedure A, plot the volume increase readings (V60) between the 
30 s and 60 s reading on a separate graph.  Generally, a part of the same graph 
is used, see Fig. 8.  For Procedure B, plot the pressure decrease reading 
between the 30 s and 60 s reading on a separate graph.  The test curve shows 
an almost straight line section within the range of either low volume increase 
readings (V60) for Procedure A or low pressure decrease for Procedure B.  In 
this range, a constant soil deformation modulus can be measured.  Past the so-
called creep pressure, plastic deformations become prevalent. 
FIG. 8     Pressuremeter Test Curves for Procedure A 
References 
Housel, W.S. (1959), “Dynamic & Static Resistance of Cohesive Soils”,  ASTM STP 254, pp. 22-23. 
Stoll, M.U.W. (1961, Discussion, Proc. 5th ICSMFE, Paris, Vol. III, pp. 279-281. 
Bourges, F. and Levillian, J-P (1988), “force portante des rideaux plans metalliques charges verticalmement,” 
Bull. No. 158, Nov.-Dec., des laboratoires des ponts et chaussees, p. 24. 
Fellenius, Bengt H. (1996), Basics of Foundation Design, BiTech Publishers Ltd., p.79.

APPENDIX G 
SHAFT STIFFNESS ESTIMATION 
LT-A  11/19/2009
1 
DETERMINATION OF PILE STIFFNESS AND LOAD 
DISTRIBUTION BY THE TANGENT AND SECANT 
STIFFNESS METHOD (October 2001) 
Introduction:  Calculations for pile stiffness values based on empirical relationships 
such as the ACI formula result in a single value of stiffness (AE).  The calculations are 
based on many assumptions, including average concrete strength and pile diameter, 
which may be only nominally correct.  In addition, confinement effects and the fact that 
the stiffness of a composite pile will change as the pile is subjected to load are not 
considered. 
This tangent stiffness analysis computes the stiffness AE directly as opposed to 
computing the modulus E and then multiplying by area A. In many cases A is also a 
variable which in general cannot be separated from the analytically determined AE 
(unless a very detailed callipering of the shaft is available). 
Assumptions:  As discussed below, this analytical method assumes that the side shear 
section between the O-cell™ and a given strain gage level has reached or at least 
approached its ultimate capacity.  Further, AE is assumed to be a linear function of 
strain ε in accord with Eq. 2: 
b
a
P
AE
[ Eq. 1 ] 
Procedure:  Tangent stiffness is taken as the “instantaneous” slope of the non-linear 
load-strain curve at a given load.  For our purposes, this is determined as the change in 
applied load divided by change in strain for each successive load increment i:  
1
i
i
1
i
i
i
P
P
P
AE
[ Eq. 2 ] 
Noting that P=  x AE dε, substituting Eq. 1 and integrating gives Eq. 3. 
C
b
a
2
1
P
2
[ Eq. 3 ] 
where the constant of integration C=0, if P=0 when ε=0. 
Because of increasing shaft shear resistance, the load increase at the strain gages will 
be less than the applied load increase during the initial part of the test.  Therefore, 
assuming negligible shear increase results in too-large values of the tangent stiffness. 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell™) TECHNOLOGY 
LT-A  11/19/2009
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell™) TECHNOLOGY 
2 
Note that this side shear reducing with strain will produce too-low values of the tangent 
stiffness.  It is only after the side shear  section between the O-cell™ and the given 
strain gage level reaches its ultimate shear capacity and remains approximately 
constant that subsequent applied load increments result in measured load and strain 
increments which give a true indication of the pile stiffness.  This behavior becomes 
apparent on a plot of tangent stiffness ∆P/∆ε vs. strain ε, where the tangent stiffness 
decreases from very high values at a small ε to a linear, nearly horizontal curve at high ε 
(see Figure 1, below).   
A line which is curve-fit through this ultimate portion of the tangent-stiffness curve  
permits determining the constants values a and b in Eq. 1, which are then used in Eq. 3 
in conjunction with the strain value εi to calculate the corresponding load Pi at load 
interval i. Note that one can also show that assuming Eq. 1 means the load Pi = (avg. 
AE over εi) × εi, and that AE= the secant stiffness over εi. Assuming AE  a constant 
over all ε means a = 0 in Eq. 1.

APPENDIX  H
LT-A  11/19/2009
The enclosed report contains the data and analysis summary for the SoniCaliper
shaft caliper, performed at HCTC Preliminary Tests (Test Shaft 1), Ewa, HI on
Monday, August 10, 2009 by Denny Kort.  The shaft was calipered from a
reference depth of 6.0 feet to a maximum depth of 65.0 feet.
The minimum concrete volume is calculated to be 110.3 yd³, based on the area of
the calipered cross-sections and a Top of Concrete depth of 9.8 feet.  (Note that
this includes theoretical volume based on a nominal shaft cross-sectional area
between depths of 65.0 feet and 65.3 feet, which was not calipered.)
Note that significant wall encroachment (greater than 6.0 inches) was calculated at
the depths listed below:

SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY

INTERPRETATION OF SONICALIPER FIELD DATA REPORT 
General: T he SoniCaliper is a profiling sonar 
device, specially adapt ed to functi on in drillin g 
fluids.  Each 360° pass generated
 with the SoniCaliper d evice produces up to o ne hundred  
twenty individual echo r eturns (profile data poin ts).  In the attached figures (profile ring plots),  
the diamond points rep resent individual profile  data points.  A circle is fitted to the  data points 
using a proprietary double-step lea
st-squares t echnique, t o approximate the cross-sect ional 
geometry of the shaft.  A center location and diameter are calculated for each profile ring circle. 
Deployment:  The  device is lowere d into the shaft excavation in  incremental depths.  At  each 
depth, a 360° sweep of the shaft wall is performed.  The device is assumed to hang vertically in 
the shaft (any deviation from verticality can be noted using onboard pitch and roll sensors).  Any 
twist in  the  device rela tive to its initial or
ientation is co mpensated by an onbo ard compass 
sensor. 
Calibration:  Because  the properties of drilling fluids v
ary widely,  a calibrat ion must be  
performed for each shaft to determine fluid wavespeed (an d thus calculate range data).  This is 
done by sel ecting a profile ring of known diameter (usua
lly, but not always the upper-most 
profile ring)  as the “calibration ring”.  The data analysis then ba
ck-calculates the fluid
wavespeed based on  the known d iameter of this ring.   The  fluid wavespeed is assumed to be 
constant over the entire column of 
fluid depth. 
Shaft Verticality:  T
o determine  
shaft verticality, a profile rin
g 
(usually, but not always the
calibration r ing) is se lected as the  
“datum ring”.  The ge ometric centers 
of the datum ring and all other profile 
rings are compared.  The “center
offset” listed on the f igures indicates 
the divergence of each profile rin
g 
center poin t from the datum ring 
center poin t.  “Encroachment” is 
presented graphically as the shaded  
area representing the p ortion of th e 
shaft wall which would encroach into 
the perfectly vertical projection of the 
datum ring t o the dept h in qu estion.  
The ma
ximum a
mount o
f 
encroachment for each profile ring is 
also given numerically. 
Calipered Volume: The cross sectional are
a of each profile ring
 is determined and 
a 
cumulative volume for the calipered portion of the shaft is calculated.  Note that this volume is a  
minimum.

APPENDIX I 
MOBILIZED NET UNIT SIDE SHEAR 
LT-A  11/19/2009
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell™) TECHNOLOGY 
INCREMENTAL CURVES OF UNIT SIDE SHEAR VS. UPWARD SHAFT MOVEMENT 
Engineers generally find that an estimate of unit side shear stress versus displacement 
is a useful deep foundation design parameter.  Typically we will report these values for 
various shaft zones versus O-cell™ bearing plate displacement.  In cases where 
significant movements occur and the ultimate capacity is approached, the displacement 
of each shear zone is very close to the measured O-cell™ plate movement.  However, 
in cases with minimal movement, O-cell™ bearing plate displacement is primarily due to 
elastic compression of the shaft.  In these cases, a more refined analysis is warranted 
to estimate the average displacement of each respective shear zone noting that that the 
shear zone adjacent to the O-cell™ will displace more than subsequent zones.  Plotting 
the unit shear values against the average shear zone displacement is more 
representative of the actual unit shear versus movement characteristics of the tested 
shaft. 
The figure(s) in this Appendix provide information about the progressive mobilization of 
unit side shear stress as it developed during the upward shear movement between the 
shaft and the soil above the O-cell™.  Separate curves provide this information for the 
following shaft zones:  Between the O-cell™ and the closest level of strain gages that 
provided usable data, between the levels of strain gages that provided usable data, and 
between the highest strain gage level with usable data and the level above which the 
side shear becomes negligible. 
Our analysis assumes an average, constant unit side shear resistance along the above 
shaft zones, and that the movement represents the average for that zone. 
The analysis uses the measured upward cell movement, the measured total elastic 
compression of the shaft, and with the computed elastic compression over each zone 
corrected to match the measured total.  We also assume that the ground surface beside 
the shaft heaves only a negligible amount, and report the net unit side shears after 
correcting for the self weight of each respective shaft zone. 
Based on tests and FE modeling, we suggest that the upward shear mobilization curves 
herein from the O-cell™ test also apply, approximately, to the downward movement 
shear mobilization from conventional top compression loading.  We suggest a 0.95 
shear correction factor for cohesionless soils.  For top tension loading we suggest a 
0.80 factor for all soils.
